⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبين لهم معالم الدين، ويرشدهم إلى الحق، ويهديهم إلى سبيل الخير، ويوقفهم على نهج العبادة لخالقهم، وأداء شكر نعمه عليهم، كانوا متحاجين إلى من يسوسهم سياسة فاضلة، ويقبض على يدي القوى وينصر الظلوم وينصفه من ظالمه، ويمكن الدهماء ويؤمن الطرق، ويطفئ نائرة الفتن الواقعة في الأرض، لا يتقاضى الممالك المؤثلة، وانحلال قوى الولايات القديمة كان ملك فارس، وكان ملوكهم أعز ملوك الأرض وأشدهم شوكة، وأقواهم بطشاً، وأكثرهم عدة وعديدة ذخائر، وأعمرهم ولاية، قد ضعف، وتكاثر أسباب الخلل في قواعده وأركانه، وتداعى إلى الانعدام ما طال وعرض من بنيانه لشؤم ما كان بملكهم أبرويز من ادعاء الألوهية حتى نهى عن ذلك على لسان مغنية فأربد فقصمه الله تعالى وسلط ابنه شيرويه عليه فقتله، ثم كان يدوم لمن استولى منهم على الملك مدة ولا يشتد له شوكة، وآل الأمر إلى نصب النساء بأمور المملكة وتفويض الأمر إلى الصبيان، وكان لا يشتغل الواحد منهم للقيام بأسباب السياسات وضبط المملكة، وامتدت الأيدي الخاطئة والأطماع الفاسدة لا إلى الرعية بالإجحاف والاستئصال. وكذا ملك اليمن انتقض باستيلاء السودان عليه، وملك الحيرة بقتل أبرويز النعمان بن المنذر، وملك آل جفته وهم الغسانيون كان آل للضعف وأشرف على البطلان والزوال، وبقى الناس بعد انتقاض هذه الممالك أو ضعف أركانها وزوال السياسة وحفظ الحوزة والذب عن حريم الرعية خائفين وجلين طالبين من الله تعالى حسن الفرج مقسمين بالله جهد أيمانهم لئن جاءهم نذير ليكونن أهدى من إحدى الأمم. فرحم الله خلقه وبعث محمدًا نبيه، وبدلهم من بعد خوفهم أمنًا، وبعد اضطراب أمورهم واختلاف أحوالهم سكونا وقرارا، وبعد انتشار الظلم في الخليقة، وتطاير شرره في أصناف البرية عدلاً وإنصافا، فمن لم يقابل هذه النعمة بحسن القبول، ولم نفسه عاجزا عن أداء الشكر، فهو الكافر الجهول الذي لا دواء لدائه إلا الحسام القاضب لا حيلة لبرئه إلا الحمام الغالب، والله ولى العصمة وكاشف الغمة.
And to clarify for them the landmarks of religion, guide them to the truth, lead them to the path of goodness, stop them at the path of worship of their Creator, and performance of gratitude for His favors upon them, they were in need of someone to govern them with a virtuous policy, and to restrain the hand of the strong, and help the oppressed and give him justice from his oppressor, and enable the common people, and secure the roads, and extinguish the flame of seditions occurring in the land, not demanding the tributary kingdoms. And the dissolution of the forces of the old states: the kingdom of Persia was, and its kings were the most honored kings of the earth, the most formidable, the mightiest in power, most abundant in military equipment and treasures, and most prosperous in rule, had weakened, and the causes of corruption multiplied in its foundations and pillars, and what had long and wide of its structure collapsed to annihilation due to the ill omen of what was in their king Abrawiz of claiming divinity until he was rebuked for that by the tongue of a songstress, so he became angry, so Allah the Exalted destroyed him and subjected his son Shiruyah to him so he killed him. Then the rule would continue for whoever seized kingship among them for a time but his power did not strengthen. And the matter ended up with the appointment of women for the affairs of the kingdom and delegating the matter to boys, and not one of them occupied himself with undertaking the means of governance and controlling the kingdom. And the wrong hands and corrupt ambitions extended, not to the subjects with oppression and extermination. Likewise, the kingdom of Yemen was corrupted by the domination of the Sudan over it, and the kingdom of Hira by the killing of al-Nu'man ibn al-Mundhir by Abrawiz, and the kingdom of the house of Jafna, who were the Ghassanids, came to weakness and was close to extinction and disappearance. After the corruption of these kingdoms or the weakening of their pillars, the disappearance of governance, the preservation of territory, and defense of the subjects' sanctity, the people remained fearful and anxious, asking Allah the Exalted for a good relief, swearing by Allah with their most solemn oaths: 'If a warner comes to them, they will surely be better guided than one of the nations.' So Allah the Exalted had mercy on His creation and sent Muhammad His prophet, and changed for them after their fear security, and after the disorder of their affairs and the difference of their conditions tranquility and stability, and after the spread of injustice in creation and the scattering of its sparks among the kinds of creatures justice and equity. So whoever does not meet this blessing with good acceptance, and does not consider himself incapable of performing gratitude, then he is the ungrateful ignorant one for whose illness there is no remedy except the sharp sword, and no cure for his recovery except inevitable death. And Allah is the Master of protection and the Remover of distress.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a historical and theological narrative. It begins by describing the need for a righteous leader who can establish justice, protect the weak, and maintain order. The author then surveys the decay of major pre-Islamic kingdoms: Persia, Yemen, Hira, and Ghassan. For Persia, he details the causes: the tyranny of King Abrawiz who claimed divinity, leading to internal strife and eventual collapse. The kingdom fell into weakness, with women and children ruling, and oppression of subjects. Similarly, Yemen fell to the Sudan, Hira was ruined by the killing of al-Nu'man, and Ghassan weakened. As a result, people lived in fear and prayed for relief, swearing that if a warner came, they would follow guidance. In response, Allah sent Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) as mercy, transforming fear into security and injustice into justice. The passage concludes by warning that rejection of this blessing leads to punishment, and only death can cure such ignorance.

Key Terms
ملك اليمن
النعمان بن المنذر
آل جفته
الغسانيون

What caused the fall of the kingdom of Hira according to the passage?
A) Invasion by Sudan
B) Murder of its king al-Nu'man by Abrawiz
C) Natural disaster
D) Internal rebellion

Key Takeaway
The collapse of multiple kingdoms left humanity in a state of fear and longing, preparing them to accept divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face crises—personal or societal—do you truly turn to Allah in desperation, or do you rely on your own plans? The people in this lesson had no hope left but Allah. What is your first refuge?
?
The people swore by Allah that if a warner came, they would be better guided than any other nation. Are you living today as if you have already received that warner? Does your life reflect the guidance of the Prophet, or have you become complacent?
?
You are in a position to know the truth of prophethood. The people in this lesson only hoped for it. Are you using this knowledge to guide your actions and character, or are you taking it for granted? What specific change will you make today to be more guided than the nations who came before?

The Sending of Prophet Muhammad and the Divine Mercy
Description of the sending of the Prophet and its effects, with a warning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah responded to the desperate state of humanity by sending Prophet Muhammad as a mercy, bringing security and justice, and the warning for those who reject this blessing.

Core Idea
Divine mercy arrives in response to sincere desperation and transforms fear into peace.

After describing the decay of kingdoms and the fearful state of the people, Imam al-Nasafi presents the divine response: Allah sent Muhammad (peace be upon him) as a mercy. This resulted in the transformation of fear into security, disorder into tranquility, and injustice into justice. The passage ends with a stern warning for those who reject this blessing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'فرحم الله خلقه' to the end. Notice the contrasts: fear to security, disorder to tranquility, injustice to justice.
2
Identify the transformation
List the pairs: fear → security, disorder → tranquility, injustice → justice and equity.
This shows the comprehensive change brought by the Prophet.
3
Understand the warning
The passage warns that rejecting this blessing leaves no cure except death. This emphasizes the seriousness of ingratitude.

Refutation of the Claim of Lost Counter-Texts to the Quran
Nasafi refutes the claim that counter-texts to the Quran existed but were lost.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that counter-texts to the Quran existed but were lost is rationally untenable based on the historical incentive to preserve them.

Core Idea
The enemies of Islam would have preserved any counter-texts, so their absence proves they never existed.

Nasafi addresses a potential objection to the miraculous nature of the Quran: that opponents produced similar texts but they were lost over time. This might seem plausible, but a closer look at the historical context reveals why it cannot be true. This lesson unpacks Nasafi's concise refutation, which hinges on the motives of the Quran's opponents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the claim that counter-texts existed but were lost due to the dominance of Islam. This sets up the problem Nasafi refutes.
Notice that the objection tries to explain away the Quran's uniqueness.
2
Analyze Nasafi's response
Nasafi calls this claim 'extreme ignorance' and presents a logical counter-argument. Pay attention to the conditional reasoning: if counter-texts existed, the enemies would have transmitted them.
3
Reflect on the historical implication
Consider why the enemies' need to transmit counter-texts was pressing. What does this argument tell us about the Quran's status as a miracle?
This connects the lesson to broader discussions of prophethood and miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن زعم الجاحدون لرسالته الله أنهم عارضوه إلا أن ذلك صار مندرسًا لعلو كلمته وشيوع دعوته وشدة شوكة أتباعه وأشياعه. قيل لهم هذا جهل مفرط، فإنهم لو عارضوه وكان المنكرون له أكثر من متبعيه وأعداؤه أوفر عددًا وأقوى يدًا من أوليائه ؛ لكان ينبغي أن ينقل أولئك المعارض كما نقل هؤلاء القرآن إذ كانت حاجتهم إلى ذلك ماسة لدفع خصومهم من
If the deniers of His messengerhood claim that they opposed him, but that became extinct due to the high status of his word, the spread of his call, and the strength of his followers and partisans, it is said to them: This is extreme ignorance. For if they opposed him, and the deniers were more numerous than his followers and his enemies more numerous in number and stronger in power than his supporters, then it would be necessary that those counter-texts be transmitted just as the Qur'an was transmitted, because their need for that was pressing to repel their opponents from
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage refutes the argument that counter-texts to the Quran existed but were lost. Nasafi calls this claim extreme ignorance (jahl mufrit). He reasons that if such counter-texts existed, the enemies of Islam, who were more numerous and powerful at the time, would have preserved them to use against the Muslims. Their need for such material to refute the Quran was urgent, so it is inconceivable that they would not have transmitted it. The fact that no such texts have come down to us proves they never existed.

Key Terms
معارضة
مندرس
جهل مفرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Historically significant texts can be accidentally lost even if they were important.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the enemies of Islam had a strong incentive to preserve any counter-Quranic texts, so their loss would be inexplicable. Therefore, the absence of such texts proves they never existed.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the claim that counter-texts to the Quran existed but were lost?
A) Because the Muslims destroyed them.
B) Because the enemies of Islam would have preserved them to refute the Quran.
C) Because such texts are impossible.
D) Because the Quran itself says they didn't exist.

Key Takeaway
The claim that counter-texts to the Quran were lost is refuted by the fact that the enemies of Islam would have preserved them if they existed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The enemies of the Prophet would have preserved any counter-text because they needed it. Is there any truth you have rejected or ignored because acknowledging it would require you to change? What would it cost you to accept it?
?
If you were one of the deniers hearing Nasafi's argument, could you honestly find a flaw? Or would you be forced to admit the claim is false? What does your hesitation reveal about your own motives?
?
The absence of evidence is not always evidence of absence—but here it is. When in your life have you used the excuse 'maybe it got lost' to avoid facing the truth? Will you continue making excuses, or will you face the evidence?

Refuting the Lost Counter with Musaylimah's Example
Rebuts claim that opponents produced a counter lost due to Prophet's strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that opponents produced a counter-Quran that was lost is invalid, and how the example of Musaylimah proves this.

Core Idea
A lost counter is no counter at all; if it existed, it would have been transmitted.

This passage addresses a common objection: that the opponents did produce a counter to the Quran but it was suppressed by the Prophet's followers. The author refutes this as absurd, arguing that the opponents had every motive to preserve such a counter, and even Musaylimah's nonsense survived. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line where the opponents claim their counter was lost due to the Prophet's power. Note the three factors they cite: loftiness of his word, spread of his call, and power of his followers.
The objection is based on alleged suppression.
2
Author's initial response
See how the author dismisses the claim as 'extreme ignorance' (جهل مفرط). This sets the tone for the logical refutation.
Notice the directness of the rebuttal.
3
Logical argument
Follow the conditional reasoning: if the opponents were more numerous and powerful, they would have transmitted their counter to refute the Prophet. The absence of transmission proves its non-existence.
This is a key proof based on historical plausibility.
4
Example of Musaylimah
Observe the analogy: Musaylimah's incoherent speech survived despite Islam's strength. If a real counter existed, it would have survived similarly.
Musaylimah's example strengthens the argument from transmission.
5
Conclusion
Synthesize that the lack of any transmitted counter confirms the Quran's inimitability and the falsehood of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن زعم الجاحدون لرسالته الله أنهم عارضوه إلا أن ذلك صار مندرسًا لعلو كلمته وشيوع دعوته وشدة شوكة أتباعه وأشياعه. قيل لهم هذا جهل مفرط، فإنهم لو عارضوه وكان المنكرون له أكثر من متبعيه وأعداؤه أوفر عددًا وأقوى يدًا من أوليائه؛ لكان ينبغي أن ينقل أولئك المعارض كما نقل هؤلاء القرآن إذ كانت حاجتهم إلى ذلك ماسة لدفع خصومهم من ألا ترى أن ما أتى به مسيلمة الكذاب من الهذيان كيف بقى وما هاجموا به رسول الله ( ) لم يرتفع ولم ينعدم لكثرة أعوان الإسلام وأنصاره، وقوة أشياع النبي وأتباعه.
For if the deniers of His messenger claim that they opposed him, but that became obliterated due to the loftiness of his word, the spread of his call, and the severity of the power of his followers and partisans—it is said to them: This is extreme ignorance. For if they had opposed him, and the deniers of him were more numerous than his followers and his enemies greater in number and stronger in hand than his supporters, then it would have been necessary that those countering be transmitted just as these transmitted the Quran, since their need for that was pressing to repel their opponents. Do you not see that what Musaylimah the liar brought of nonsense—how it remained? And what they attacked the Messenger of God with did not rise nor become extinct, despite the abundance of the supporters of Islam and its helpers, and the strength of the partisans and followers of the Prophet.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that they did produce a counter to the Quran but it was lost because the Prophet's followers suppressed it. The author refutes this as extreme ignorance. He argues that if a counter had existed, it would have been transmitted by the opponents because they needed it to refute the Prophet, and the opponents were initially more numerous and powerful. Moreover, the author points to the example of Musaylimah the false prophet: his incoherent speech survived despite the strength of Islam. So if the opponents' alleged counter had existed, it would have survived as well. Its lack of transmission proves it never existed.

Key Terms
مندرِس
معارض
مسيلمة
هذيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the lack of a recorded counter to the Quran could be due to suppression rather than the absence of any such counter.
Correction: The author argues that if a counter existed, the opponents, who were initially more numerous, would have preserved and transmitted it to refute the Prophet. The survival of Musaylimah's speech shows that even weak speech can be transmitted if it serves a purpose. Therefore, the absence of transmission proves no counter was ever produced.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use the example of Musaylimah the liar?
A) To show that false prophets are easily forgotten.
B) To demonstrate that even incoherent speech survives if it is widely transmitted.
C) To argue that the Quran's inimitability is due to its content.
D) To prove that the Prophet's followers suppressed all opposition.

Key Takeaway
The claim of a suppressed counter is baseless; the survival of Musaylimah's nonsense proves that any true counter would have been transmitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Musaylimah's nonsense survived—what does that mean for your own legacy? What will survive of your deeds, and are you building something that will stand the test of time?
?
The enemies had every reason to preserve a counter, yet none exists. How does this challenge your trust in the Quran's preservation? What does it demand from you in response?

The Challenge to Produce a Surah and the Refutation of Fear
Refutation of the fear excuse for not meeting the Quran's challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the excuse of fear as a reason for not producing a surah like the Quran.

Core Idea
If opponents already risk death by denying the Prophet, fear cannot excuse them from the challenge.

Having presented the Quran's challenge to produce a surah like it, the author now addresses a possible excuse: fear of reprisal from the Muslim community. He argues that this excuse is inconsistent because the opponents have already incurred the death penalty by their open denial. Moreover, historical figures like Ibn al-Rawandi openly attacked Islam without being stopped. This lesson examines this powerful rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author sets up the challenge, then anticipates the excuse of fear.
Pay attention to the rhetorical structure: challenge → excuse → refutation → examples.
2
Identify the inconsistency
The author points out that the opponents already speak against the Prophet and reject the argument, which itself warrants death. So why would they fear death from producing a counter?
3
Consider the secret option
If they claim to do it secretly, the author retorts that they can produce the counter secretly as well. This closes the loophole.
4
Examine the historical examples
Ibn al-Rawandi and Ibn Zakariyya wrote books attacking Islam without being prevented by fear. This proves that fear is not a real barrier.
These examples strengthen the argument by showing that others did it.

Then you are the addressees of the Almighty's saying: 'Then bring a Surah like it' (2:23), demanded by that and argued against by it. So bring you its like and oppose it with its similar. If you claim that you fear the guardians of the religion who defend it with swords and spears, and the common people who hasten to shed blood of one known to have disparaged the religion they follow, it is said to you: What is the matter with you? Commentary
This passage is a direct challenge to the opponents of the Quran. The author first notes that the opponents are well-equipped with eloquence, sciences, and wisdom, so they should be able to produce a surah like the Quran, as the Quran itself challenges them. If they claim fear of the Muslim community's swords as an excuse, he points out the inconsistency: they already openly slander the Prophet and reject the proof, which already incurs the death penalty, so why not produce a counter? If they say they do it secretly, then they can produce a counter secretly too. The author then cites historical examples: Ibn al-Rawandi and Ibn Zakariyya al-Razi (the physician) openly wrote books attacking religion without being prevented by fear, so the excuse of fear is invalid.

Refuting the Fear Excuse for the Qur'an's Inimitability
Nasafi refutes the fear excuse for not rivaling the Qur'an with historical counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fear excuse for not producing a rival to the Qur'an is invalid, and how historical examples demonstrate that opponents had no fear but still failed.

Core Idea
Opponents failed to rival the Qur'an not from fear, but from sheer inability.

This passage tackles a common objection: that no one produced a rival to the Qur'an because they feared the Muslims' swords. Nasafi counters by pointing out that opponents openly attacked Islam in other ways without fear, yet never produced a rival. Their true reason was inability, not fear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the confident tone as Nasafi dismantles the excuse.
Pay attention to the examples—they are the core of the refutation.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The passage moves from a general observation (Musaylima's nonsense persisted) to a direct challenge, then refutes the fear excuse with historical examples, and concludes with inability as the real reason.
This is a typical kalām style: assertion, objection, refutation.
3
Reflect on the proof's strength
Ask yourself: Why does this argument seal the case for the Qur'an's inimitability? The examples show that even those who wrote whole books attacking Islam did not dare to produce a single comparable surah. That is the proof of 'ajz (inability).
This connects to the broader doctrine of i'jāz al-Qur'ān.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا ترى أن ما أتى به مسيلمة الكذاب من الهذيان كيف بقى وما هاجموا به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يرتفع ولم ينعدم لكثرة أعوان الإسلام وأنصاره وقوة أشياع النبي وأتباعه. ثم نقول لهم: أنتم لن يعوزكم الكتبة البلغاء والخطباء والفصحاء، وفضلتم سادة العرب بضمكم إلى فصاحة العرب أنواع العلوم واشتغالكم بدرسها طول عمركم، وإحرازكم حكمة الهند وفلسفة اليونان وسياسة العجم، ثم أنتم المخاطبون بقوله تعالى: {فَأْتُوا بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِّثْلِهِ} مطالبون بذلك محجوجون به، فأتوا أنتم بمثله وعارضوه بشكله، فإن زعمتم أنكم تخافون الحفاظ للملة الذابين عنها بالسيوف والرماح والعامة المتسارعين إلى إراقة الدماء من عرفوا منه القدح في الدين الذي يدينون به. قيل لكم ما لكم تجاسرتم على إظهار المخالفة وبسط اللسان في الرسول بالتكذيب، وفي الحجة بالجحود والرد واستحقاقكم القتل واستيجابكم إراقة الدماء به لا بإظهار ما يعارض الحجة ويقابل الدليل؟ فإن قلتم: إنا نظهر المقالة سراً. قيل لكم: فأظهروا المعارض أيضاً سراً. ثم نقول ما بال ابن الراوندي لم تمنعه هيبة الذابين عن الدين الناصرين له عن تصنيف كتاب التاج، وكتاب الزمرد وقضيب الذهب، وابن زكريا المتطبب لم يمنعه من ذلك عن تصنيف كتاب مخاريق الأنبياء وكتاب نقض الأديان، وجماعة التنويه بالبصرة لم يمنعه من تصانيف لهم كثيرة في نصرة قولهم بأصلين قديمين للعالم والطعن في الإسلام بل عن المجاهرة في المناظرة والمقابلة لدى المجادلة والاسفراري لم يمنعه ذلك عما صنف من أنواع الإلحاد وجماعة الباطنية لم يمنعهم ذلك عما صنفوا من الكتب لنصرة نحلتهم الرديئة وثلبهم الدين الظاهر كأبي حاتم الرازي والكوسج البزدوي والجاورساني وأبي علي وغيرهم ممن يطول ذكرهم حتى اشتهر كل ذلك، وانتشر في أداني بلدان الإسلام وأقاصيها، وبلغ جميع أطراف الأرض ونواحيها، والخلعاء والمجان لم يمنعهم ذلك من هجو الإسلام وشهر رمضان والصيام والصلاة وأشباه ذلك ومنعهم عن المعارضة وهي أشفى لصدورهم، وأشد تحصيلاً لفرضهم ومقصودهم، بل امتنعوا عن ذلك لعجزهم وقصورهم، فلزمتهم الحجة وانقطعت معذرتهم في الإعراض عنه؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، عجزوا عن ذلك لأنه كان أفصحهم لسانًا وأجودهم بيانًا على ما روى عنه عليه السلام أنه قال: "أنا أفصح العرب"، وذلك لا يدل.
Do you not see that what Musaylima the liar brought of nonsense persisted, and what they attacked the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) with did not rise nor disappear due to the many supporters of Islam and its helpers and the strength of the followers of the Prophet and his companions? Then we say to them: You do not lack scribes, eloquent speakers, orators, and eloquent poets; and you have surpassed the masters of the Arabs by adding to the eloquence of the Arabs the types of sciences, your occupation with their study throughout your lives, and your acquisition of the wisdom of India, the philosophy of the Greeks, and the politics of the Persians. Then you are the ones addressed by His statement: 'Then bring a surah like it'—you are required by it and refuted by it. So bring its like and oppose it with its match. If you claim that you fear the protectors of the religion who defend it with swords and spears and the common people who hasten to shed blood, of those who know from him (the critic) a slander against the religion they profess, it is said to you: What is the matter with you that you dare to show opposition and extend your tongue against the Messenger by denial, and against the proof by rejection and refusal, deserving killing and meriting the shedding of blood by that, and not by showing what opposes the proof and confronts the evidence? If you say: We show the statement secretly, it is said to you: Then show the opposition also secretly. Then we say: What is the matter with Ibn al-Rawandi that the awe of the defenders of the religion, the helpers of it, did not prevent him from composing the Book of the Crown, the Book of the Emerald, and the Golden Rod? And Ibn Zakariyya the physician was not prevented by that from composing the Book of the Tricks of the Prophets and the Book of the Refutation of Religions. And the group of al-Tanwih in Basra was not prevented from having many compositions in support of their statement of two ancient origins for the world and the attack on Islam, rather from open disputation and confrontation in debate. And al-Isfarari was not prevented by that from what he composed of types of heresy. And the group of al-Batiniyya were not prevented by that from what they composed of books to support their vile sect and their reviling of the apparent religion, like Abu Hatim al-Razi, and al-Kusaj al-Bazdawi, and al-Jawarsani, and Abu Ali, and others whose mention would be lengthy, until all that became famous and spread in the near and far cities of Islam and reached all parts of the earth and its regions. And the lowlifes and the mad were not prevented by that from satirizing Islam, the month of Ramadan, fasting, prayer, and the like of that; but they were prevented from opposition, which is more fulfilling for their hearts and more effective in achieving their purpose and intention. Rather, they refrained from it due to their inability and insufficiency; so the proof became binding on them and their excuse in turning away from it was cut off. If they say: Yes, they were unable to do that because he (the Prophet) was the most eloquent of them in tongue and the most excellent in expression, according to what was narrated from him (peace be upon him) that he said: 'I am the most eloquent of the Arabs,' and that does not indicate (the inability).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the excuse that opponents of the Prophet were prevented from producing a rival to the Qur'an because they feared the Muslim defenders and the common people. Nasafi begins by noting the persistence of Musaylima's nonsense compared to the Prophet's challenge, indicating that if the opponents had a true rival, it would have remained. He then addresses the opponents directly: they have eloquent scribes, orators, and scholars; they surpass the Arabs in eloquence and learning; yet they are challenged by the Qur'anic verse 'then bring a surah like it.' They are obligated and refuted by this challenge. If they claim fear of death or harm, Nasafi points out that they openly engage in other forms of attack on the Prophet and Islam, which also incur the death penalty, so why do they not produce a rival if they are able? If they say they do these things secretly, then they can also produce a rival secretly. The author then provides historical examples: heretics like Ibn al-Rawandi and Ibn Zakariyya wrote books attacking Islam (e.g., 'The Tricks of the Prophets', 'The Refutation of Religions') without fear, yet they did not produce a rival to the Qur'an. Similarly, groups like al-Tanwih in Basra, al-Isfarari, and numerous Batini theologians authored explicit works of heresy and revilement of Islam, spreading far and wide. Even lowlifes and madmen openly mocked Islam, Ramadan, fasting, and prayer. Despite this lack of fear, they were prevented from producing a rival—not by fear, but by their sheer inability. Thus the argument (hujja) is established against them and their excuse is cut off. Finally, Nasafi anticipates the objection that the Prophet's superior eloquence made it impossible, but he notes this assertion itself does not prove inability, leaving the argument open. The passage is a classic proof of the inimitability (i'jāz) of the Qur'an, showing that fear does not explain the lack of rival attempts; only incapacity does.

Key Terms
حجة
عجز
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opponents could not rival the Qur'an because they feared Muslim reprisal.
Correction: Historical evidence shows opponents openly wrote books attacking Islam and the Prophet without fear, yet never produced a rival, proving their failure was due to inability, not fear.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the examples of Ibn al-Rawandi and other heretics in this passage?
A) To show that these heretics were righteous scholars.
B) To argue that if they had been able to produce a rival to the Qur'an, they would have done so despite their fearlessness in writing against Islam.
C) To demonstrate that the Muslims were too weak to stop them.
D) To prove that the Qur'an is not a miracle.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's inimitability is proven by the fact that opponents, who freely attacked Islam in other ways, never produced a rival—showing their inability, not their fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are confronted with a clear command or challenge from Allah (like the Qur'an's challenge), do you find yourself making excuses to avoid responding? What is the real reason behind your hesitation?
?
The opponents openly attacked Islam in other ways, but never produced a rival surah. Reflect on your own life: what 'rivals' do you entertain that compete with the Qur'an in your heart? Are you truly unable to prioritize it, or do you hide behind excuses?
?
If you were to honestly assess your engagement with the Qur'an, would your actions show that you believe it is from Allah or that you treat it as just another book? What would change if you truly admitted your inability to match its words and submitted fully?

The Miraculous Eloquence of the Qur'an and the Impossibility of Human Origin
Nasafi argues that the Qur'an's sublime style and the Prophet's unlettered state prove its divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'an's profound theological content and unmatched eloquence constitute a miracle (i'jaz), especially given the Prophet's unlettered background.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's style and depth prove it cannot be from a man, let alone an unlettered one.

Imam Nasafi presents a compelling argument for the miraculous nature of the Qur'an. He first highlights the Qur'an's unparalleled eloquence and depth, then shows that even the most learned theologians have not exhausted its meanings. Finally, he argues that it is impossible for an unlettered man from an ignorant environment to have produced such profound arguments. This proves the Qur'an is from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two main parts: the description of the Qur'an's style and the argument about the Prophet's background.
Notice the powerful language Nasafi uses to describe the Qur'an's effect on the intellect.
2
Understand the two-part argument
Identify how Nasafi first contrasts the Qur'an's speech with human speech, then presents the impossibility of an unlettered man producing such content.
The second part is a reductio ad absurdum: if the Prophet had produced it, he would have needed knowledge he could not have acquired.
3
Reflect on the implication
Recognize that this argument establishes the Qur'an as a miracle (i'jaz) and confirms the truth of the Prophet's message.
This argument is a standard proof of prophethood in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن الله تعالى لا يخرج كلامه مخرج كلام الواحد منا إذا ناظر خصمه بل يخرج مخرج مخاطبة السادة العبيد، والأرباب المماليك، فأخرج هذه المعاني على هذا النظم العجيب والوصف البليغ الذي تكاد العقول الوافرة، والألباب الكاملة تتحير في رونقه وفخامته وبلاغته وفصاحته، بل تذهل من هيبته وجلالته، ثم إن المتكلمين من سلف الأمة منذ تكلموا في هذه الخصوم وأثبتوا دلائلها فى التصاريف لم يزيدوا على ما في الكتاب من مع الإشارة إلى ذلك، بل لم يستخرجوا من قعور تلك البحور وإن غاصت فيها أفكارهم هو المسائل عشر ما فيها من درر الحجج وغرر المعاني والنكت ثم من الممتنع الذي لا تصور لثبوته ولا يطلق العقل إخطاره بالقلب أن يخرج رجل أمي بلغ أشده، ومضى أربعين سنة من عمره في قوم لم يكن لهم من العلم بدلالة الكلام وحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع وتوحيده وثبوت صفاته والبعث بعد الموت حظ، ولم يخطر ببال أحد منهم من ذلك، شيء، لم يقرعوا للتوحيد بابا، ويجعلون الأنداد والأصنام شيئًا عجابًا، لم يختلف إلى أحد للتعلم ولا درس كتابًا فيه شيء من ذلك ثم عرف من تلقاء نفسه جميع هذه الوجوه التي مر ذكرها بدلائلها، ثم بين على وجه قصر
But Allah the Exalted does not bring out His speech in the manner of speech of one of us when he debates his opponent. Rather, He brings it out in the manner of addressing masters to slaves and lords to servants. So He brought out these meanings in this wondrous composition and eloquent description at which abundant intellects and perfect minds almost become bewildered in its elegance, grandeur, eloquence, and clarity; indeed they become dazzled by its awe and majesty. Then indeed the theologians from the predecessors of the community, ever since they spoke about these disputations and established their proofs in the treatises, did not add anything beyond what is in the Book beyond indicating to that. Rather, they did not extract from the depths of those seas, even if their thoughts dived into them, one tenth of what is in them of pearls of proofs, best of meanings, and subtleties. Then it is impossible—which has no mental representation of its existence and the intellect does not allow its occuring in the heart—that an unlettered man, who reached his maturity and passed forty years of his life among people who had no share of knowledge of the indications of speech (kalam), origination of the world, establishment of the Maker, His unity, establishment of His attributes, and resurrection after death—and not a single thought of any of that occurred to any of them; they did not knock on any door for tawhid; they considered rivals and idols a wondrous thing; he did not attend any teacher nor study a book containing any of that—then he came to know from himself all these aspects that have been mentioned with their proofs, then he explained in a concise manner...
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the Qur'an is of human origin. The author first describes the sublime style of the Qur'anic discourse, comparing it to the speech of a master to a slave. Then he notes that even the most learned theologians have not exhausted its depths. Finally, he presents a reductio ad absurdum: it is impossible that an unlettered man who grew up in a completely ignorant environment could have produced such profound theological arguments. This proves the Qur'an is a miracle and the Prophet is truthful.

Key Terms
إعجاز
بلاغة
نظم
رجل أمي
دلائل الكلام
حدوث العالم
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet (saw) must have learned from scholars or traveled to gain knowledge to produce the Qur'an.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that the Prophet grew up in a society completely ignorant of theological arguments, never attended a teacher, and was unlettered, making it impossible for him to produce the Qur'an on his own.

Checkpoint
What makes it impossible that the Prophet (saw) produced the Qur'anic arguments himself?
A) He was illiterate and had no teachers
B) He traveled to learn from monks
C) He was known as a poet
D) He borrowed from Greek philosophy

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's unparalleled eloquence and profound teachings, combined with the Prophet's unlettered and uneducated environment, prove that the Qur'an is a divine miracle and not of human origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly treat the Qur'an as the speech of the Master addressing you, or do you read it like any other book? What would change if you read every verse knowing it comes directly from Allah with majesty and purpose?
?
The Prophet, unlettered and in an ignorant environment, produced a book that even the greatest scholars cannot match. When you encounter doubt, do you let this reality settle in your heart, or do you brush it aside? What would it take for you to be utterly convinced that the Qur'an is divine?
?
If the Qur'an contains depths that scholars have barely scratched, how much of its wisdom have you personally extracted? Are you content with superficial reading, or will you commit to diving into its proofs and letting them transform your life?

The Impossibility of Prophet's Knowledge Without Revelation
The author argues that the Prophet's knowledge could only be from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the Prophet's knowledge must be from God, based on his unlettered background and the depth of his teachings.

Core Idea
An unlettered man cannot independently know deep theology; his knowledge must be from God.

The author presents a powerful impossibility argument to demonstrate that the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ could not have acquired his profound knowledge through human means. He sets up a scenario that the mind cannot accept as possible: an unlettered man, raised in a pagan society with no exposure to theological concepts, suddenly knows and expounds the most intricate truths about God, creation, and resurrection. This bridge prepares you to read the full argument and see how it leads to the conclusion that such knowledge must be divinely given.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once through without pausing. Let the argument's flow and rhetorical force sink in. Then read the English translation.
Notice the strong language: 'ممتنع', 'محال ممتنع'.
2
Identify the impossible scenario
Scan the passage for the description of what is impossible. List the conditions: the Prophet was unlettered, forty years old, among pagans with no knowledge of kalam, etc.
The author piles up descriptors to make the impossibility vivid.
3
Understand the conclusion
After reading the impossible scenario, find the conclusion starting with 'وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة'. The author states that this impossibility proves the knowledge came from God.
The conclusion is not just that it's impossible, but that it proves divine origin.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this argument is effective. It uses common sense: if someone knows something without any apparent means, the source must be supernatural. This is a rational proof for prophethood.
This argument is a classic in kalam for proving the Prophet's truthfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الممتنع الذي لا تصور لثبوته ولا يطلق العقل إخطاره بالقلب أن يخرج رجل أمي بلغ أشده، ومضى أربعين سنة من عمره في قوم لم يكن لهم من العلم بدلالة الكلام وحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع وتوحيده وثبوت صفاته والبعث بعد الموت حظ، ولم يخطر ببال أحد منهم من ذلك شيء، لم يقرعوا للتوحيد بابا، ويجعلون الأنداد والأصنام شيئًا عجابًا، لم يختلف إلى أحد للتعلم ولا درس كتابًا فيه شيء من ذلك ثم عرف من تلقاء نفسه جميع هذه الوجوه التي مر ذكرها بدلائلها، ثم بين على وجه قصر عن بلوغ كنهه ونهايته ممن لا يحصون كثرة من العلماء المبرزين المخصوصين برجاحة العقول والألباب وحدة الخواطر وجودة القرائح الذين زجوا أعمارهم في تعلم العلوم الملية والارتياض بالمعالم الحكمية، والبحث عن حقائق الأشياء، واكتساب العلم بقوانين الجدل وشرائط البرهان. هذا محال ممتنع. وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة يعلم أنه علم ما علم من عند العزيز الحميد الذي يكرم من يشاء من عباده (بما يشاء) (1) ويعلم من يشاء ما يشاء لا يمانع في تدبيره ولا يغالب في سلطانه، ولا يدافع في مشيئته، ولا يعاز في تنفيذ قضيته، له البسط والقبض والإبرام والنقض، يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد، وهو ولي التوفيق والتسديد.
Then among that which is impossible, the actuality of which has no conceptualization and the mind does not permit its occurrence in the heart, is that an unlettered man who reached his maturity and spent forty years of his life among a people who had no share of knowledge of the indication of speech (kalam), the temporal origination of the world, the establishment of the Maker, His unity, the establishment of His attributes, and resurrection after death — and none of that crossed the mind of any one of them — they did not knock on the door of unity, and they regarded idols and images as something astonishing; he did not frequent anyone for learning nor did he study a book containing anything of that; then he knew from himself all these aspects that have been mentioned with their proofs; then he expounded in a manner falling short of reaching its depth and utmost extent for countless numbers of distinguished scholars, possessed of soundness of intellects and minds, sharpness of thoughts, and excellence of natural dispositions, who spent their lives in learning the religious sciences and exercising in the philosophical disciplines, investigating the realities of things, acquiring knowledge of the rules of dialectic and the conditions of demonstration. This is impossible and absurd. And by stopping at this statement, it is known that he knew what he knew from the Mighty, the Praised, who honors whom He wills of His servants (with what He wills) (1) and teaches whom He wills what He wills. There is no resistance to His governance, no one overcomes His authority, no one defends against His will, no one opposes the execution of His decree. To Him belong expansion and contraction, binding and loosing. He does what He wills and judges what He wants. And He is the guardian of success and guidance.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful argument for the divine origin of the Prophet's knowledge. The author argues that it is impossible (mumtani') for an unlettered man, raised in a pagan environment with no knowledge of theological concepts (such as the world's temporality, God's existence, unity, attributes, resurrection) and no teachers or books, to have known all these profound matters by himself. Moreover, he expounded them in a way that even surpasses the capabilities of learned scholars who dedicated their lives to these sciences. The author concludes that this is impossible, and therefore the Prophet's knowledge must be from God. The concluding paragraph reaffirms that God grants knowledge to whom He wills and that His decree is irresistible.

توحيد
البعث

Misconception: Some might think the Prophet learned from his society or studied with teachers before revelation.
Correction: The argument emphasizes that he was unlettered, lived among a pagan people with no such knowledge, and never studied, making it impossible that he learned from human sources.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the impossibility argument regarding the Prophet?
A) That it is impossible for an unlettered man to learn from teachers.
B) That it is impossible for an unlettered man to know such profound theological truths without divine revelation.
C) That it is impossible for scholars to understand the Qur'an.
D) That it is impossible for the Prophet to be a liar.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's unlettered background and the depth of his teachings prove that his knowledge could only come from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that you could arrive at the truth about God through your own intellect alone? This passage shows that even the Prophet, with unparalleled intelligence, needed revelation. Where, then, does your own certainty stand—on revelation or on your own reasoning? Will you now consciously rely on the Qur'an and Sunnah as your sole source of divine knowledge?
?
The Prophet spent forty years among a people completely ignorant of tawheed. How much of the ignorance around you about God have you absorbed without realizing it? Today, what step will you take to cleanse your understanding of God from any ideas that come from this world rather than from revelation?
?
If the Prophet’s knowledge could only come from God, then every teaching he brought is from God. Is there any teaching of the Prophet that you find hard to accept in your heart? Confront that reluctance now: will you submit to it as divine, or will you remain in doubt?

Belief in Angels: Honored Servants of Allah
Imam Nasafi discusses belief in angels and refutes extremes regarding them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic stance on angels as honored servants of Allah and the importance of avoiding extremes of deification and degradation.

Core Idea
Angels are honored servants of Allah, neither divine nor degraded.

Belief in angels is a fundamental pillar of Islamic faith. In this passage, Imam Nasafi grounds this belief in the Qur'an and clarifies the balanced Islamic view. He warns against two extreme positions: that of the idolaters who considered angels daughters of Allah, and that of those who diminish their status.

Guided Path
1
Grounding belief in the Qur'an
Read the verses cited by Imam Nasafi and understand that belief in angels is obligatory.
Verses from Surah al-Baqarah and Surah an-Nisa are referenced.
2
Understanding the nature of angels
Examine the description of angels as honored servants who do not precede Allah in speech and act by His command.
They never tire or get arrogant in worship.
3
Avoiding extremes
Identify the two extremes mentioned: ghuluww (exaggeration) and taqsir (deficiency), and see how the Muslim position is the middle path.
The idolaters claimed angels are daughters of Allah; some others degrade their status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيا : الإيمان بالملائكة وأما الإيمان بالملائكة فقد ورد به على ما نطق به الكتاب بقوله تعالى: ﴿ ءَامَنَ الرَّسُولُ ﴾ الآية ، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَامِنُوا بِاللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ ﴾ الآية وجرد القول أنهم : عِبَادٌ مُكْرَمُونَ لَا يَسْقُونَهُ بِالْقَوْلِ وَهُمْ بِأَمْرِهِ يَعْمَلُونَ لَا يَسْتَكْبِرُونَ عَنْ عِبَادَتِهِ وَلَا يَسْتَحْسِرُونَ وصان الله عقائد أمته عليه السلام فيهم عن الغلو الذي دان به عبدة الأصنام أنهم بنات الله والتقصير الذي ذهب إليه.
Second: Belief in angels. As for belief in angels, it has been conveyed as the Book states with His saying: 'The Messenger believes...' the verse, and His saying: 'O you who have believed, believe in Allah and His Messenger...' the verse, and he made the statement explicit that they are honored servants who do not precede Him in speech and they act by His command; they are not arrogant towards His worship nor do they tire. And Allah preserved the beliefs of his (peace be upon him) nation regarding them from the extremism which the idolaters adopted that they are daughters of Allah, and the deficiency which was held.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage addresses the second major pillar of creed: belief in angels (al-iman bi al-mala'ikah). The Imam cites Qur'anic verses (Surah al-Baqarah 2:285 and Surah an-Nisa 4:136) as evidence for the obligation to believe in angels. He then describes their nature as 'honored servants' (ibad mukramun) who do not speak before Allah and act on His command, never becoming arrogant or weary (citing Surah al-Anbiya 21:26-27, 19). The passage then highlights the balanced position of Muslims: they avoid the extremism (ghuluww) of idolaters who claimed angels are daughters of Allah, and also avoid the deficiency (taqsir) of those who deny or degrade their status. The phrase 'wa sanna Allah 'aqa'ida ummatihi' indicates that Allah protected the Muslim community from both extremes. This illustrates the principle of moderation (wasatiyya) in Islamic creed.

الغلو

بنات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels are divine or are degraded beings.
Correction: Angels are honored servants of Allah, neither divine nor demeaned, as described in the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
What does this passage mention as an extreme belief about angels?
A) That they are Allah's daughters
B) That they are purely spiritual
C) That they are immortal
D) That they were created from light

Key Takeaway
Belief in angels is obligatory, and the Islamic position is balanced: they are honored servants, neither divine nor demeaned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if the angels are recording every deed, every word, and every thought? What would you change today if you really believed that?
?

?
If angels never tire of worship, how does your own worship compare? What step will you take this week to show more consistency in your obedience to Allah?

Faith in Angels: Quranic Proof and Correct Belief
Author establishes faith in angels from Qur'an and describes the balanced belief avoiding excess and deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for belief in angels and the balanced Islamic position that avoids both the exaggeration of idolaters and the deficiency of the distorters of scripture.

Core Idea
Angels are honored servants of Allah, neither divine nor fallible.

The Qur'an commands belief in the angels and describes their nature as obedient servants. This lesson examines the revealed proofs and explains how Islam steers a middle course between those who deified angels and those who diminished their status.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proofs
Read the Arabic passage once. It begins by citing two verses that command belief in angels. Then it gives the absolute description of angels from the Qur'an.
Notice that the author uses the word 'وجرد' meaning 'made absolute'.
2
Understand the correct description of angels
From the quoted verses, identify the attributes of angels: they are honored servants, do not precede God in speech, act by His command, and do not become arrogant or weary.
These attributes are the core of Islamic angelology.
3
Learn how correct belief protects against extremes
The passage contrasts two corrupt views: the exaggeration of idolaters and the deficiency of the Jews. See how the correct belief avoids both.
The Jews' view is specified: they believed a single angel could commit kufr and be punished by metamorphosis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإيمان بالملائكة فقد ورد به على ما نطق به الكتاب بقوله تعالى: ﴿ آمَنَ الرَّسُولُ ﴾ الآية، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا آمِنُوا بِاللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ ﴾ الآية وجرد القول أنهم : ﴿ عِبَادٌ مُكْرَمُون لا يَسْبَقُونَهُ بِالْقَوْلِ وَهُمْ بِأَمْرِهِ يَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ ﴿ لَا يَسْتَكْبِرُونَ عَنْ عِبَادَتِهِ وَلَا يَسْتَحْسِرُونَ ﴾ وصان الله عقائد أمته عليه السلام فيهم عن الغلو الذي دان به عبدة الأصنام أنهم بنات الله والتقصير الذي ذهب إليه المحرفون للكتاب من اليهود أن الواحد ما لواحد منهم قد يرتكب الكفر ويعاقبه الله تعالى بالمسخ.
As for faith in the angels, it has been reported according to what the Book stated with His saying: {The Messenger believes} the verse, and His saying: {O you who have believed, believe in Allah and His Messenger} the verse. And he made the statement absolute that they are {honored servants who do not precede Him in speech and they act by His command} {they do not become arrogant in worshiping Him nor do they become weary.} And God preserved the beliefs of his community, peace be upon him, concerning them from the exaggeration which the idol worshipers held, that they are daughters of God, and from the deficiency which the distorters of the Book from the Jews went to, that one of them (a single angel) may commit disbelief and God punishes him by metamorphosis.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on faith in angels (al-īmān bi-l-malā'ika). It begins by citing Qur'anic proofs: the verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:285) where the Messenger and believers affirm faith in angels, and Surah al-Nisa (4:136) commanding believers to believe in God, His Messenger, His Books, and His angels. Then it states the correct description of angels from Qur'an 21:26-27 and 21:19: they are honored servants, they do not precede God in speech, they act by His command, and they do not become arrogant or weary. Finally, it explains that God protected the beliefs of the Muslim Ummah from two extremes: (1) the exaggeration (ghuluww) of the pagan Arabs who claimed angels are God's daughters, and (2) the deficiency (taqsīr) of the Jews who distorted scripture, claiming that an individual angel might commit kufr and be punished by being transformed into an animal. Thus the correct belief is a middle path: angels are obedient servants, not divine or fallible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels are either divine (like God's daughters) or they are fallible beings who can commit sins.
Correction: The Qur'an describes angels as honored servants who always obey, never preceding Allah in speech. They are not divine, nor do they disobey. This middle path avoids both extremes.

Checkpoint

D) They denied angels entirely

Key Takeaway
Islam teaches that angels are obedient servants of Allah, a belief that avoids the extremes of deification or degrading them as fallible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the correct belief about angels avoids two extremes: exaggeration and deficiency. Look at your own heart—do you ever find yourself attributing almost divine powers to angels, secretly hoping they can intercede for you without Allah's permission? Or do you neglect them, rarely pondering their role as obedient servants? Before you sleep tonight, repent of any subtle shirk and resolve to recite the verse 'They are honored servants who do not precede Him in speech' as a daily reminder.
?
The Qur'an describes angels as acting only by Allah's command, never speaking before Him. In contrast, how often do you rush to speak or act without seeking Allah's guidance first? Commit this week to pause before every decision—big or small—and recite 'I seek refuge in Allah from the accursed shaytan' to train your soul to align with His command.

Faith in the Messengers: Avoiding Extremes
Author enumerates three Jewish/Christian errors and the Muslim middle path

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic position on prophets as chosen and infallible servants, and how it avoids both slander and deification.

Core Idea
Muslims respect all prophets as sinless servants, neither slandering nor worshipping them.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' explains the Muslim creed regarding the prophets. It emphasizes that God protected the Muslim community from two extremes: ghuluww (exaggeration) and taqsir (deficiency). The Qur'an describes prophets as chosen and infallible. The author then contrasts the Muslim stance with Jewish and Christian distortions, showing how Muslims maintain the middle path.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the English translation to understand the meaning. Notice the structure: a general statement followed by specific examples.
The passage uses a contrastive style: 'so they did not... nor did they...'
2
Identify the two extremes
The passage mentions 'ghuluww' (exaggeration) and 'taqsir' (deficiency). Find these in the text and see how they apply to the examples given.
Exaggeration is elevating prophets to divinity; deficiency is slandering them or denying their prophethood.
3
Analyze the three examples
The passage lists three errors: Jews slandering Lot, Jews denying Abraham's prophethood, and Christians exaggerating Jesus (and Jews exaggerating Ezra). Notice how each example illustrates either deficiency or exaggeration.
Muslims avoid all these by affirming that prophets are chosen and sinless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الإيمان بالرسل ورد به وكرر ذكرهم في مواضع من كتابه، وصان الله تعالى عقائد أهل ملته عن الغلو فيهم والتقصير فقال : إنهم عباد مصطفون وخيار معصومون، فما نسبوا أحدًا منهم إلى الفجور كنسبة اليهود لوطًا إلى الفجور بابنته في حال السكر ، ولا جحدوا أحدًا منهم كجحود اليهود نبوة إبراهيم عليه السلام مع جلال قدره ورتبته في الأنبياء عليهم السلام، واقتصارهم على القول أنه كان رجلاً صالحًا، ولا ادعوا في أحد منهم ما ادعته النصارى في عيسى واليهود في عزير عليهما السلام.
And similarly, faith in the messengers has been reported and He repeated their mention in places of His Book. And God Almighty protected the beliefs of the people of His religion from exaggeration concerning them and from deficiency. So He said: They are chosen servants and select infallible ones. So they (the Muslims) did not attribute any of them to immorality, like the attribution of the Jews to Lot of immorality with his daughter in a state of drunkenness, nor did they deny any of them, like the denial of the Jews of the prophethood of Abraham, peace be upon him, despite the majesty of his rank and station among the prophets, peace be upon them, and their limiting themselves to saying that he was a righteous man. Nor did they claim for any of them what the Christians claimed for Jesus and the Jews claimed for Ezra, peace be upon them.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on faith in the messengers (al-īmān bi-l-rusul). It begins by stating that this belief is commanded in the Qur'an, which repeatedly mentions the prophets. Then it explains that God protected the Muslim community from two extremes: ghuluww (exaggeration) and taqsīr (deficiency) regarding the prophets. The correct belief, as stated in the Qur'an (not quoted directly but implied), is that they are chosen servants (ʿibād muṣṭafūn) and select infallible ones (khiyār maʿṣūmūn). The author then gives examples of wrong beliefs from Jews and Christians: (1) The Jews attributed immorality to Prophet Lot by claiming he committed incest with his daughter while drunk. (2) The Jews denied the prophethood of Abraham, limiting him to being merely a righteous man. (3) The Christians exaggerated by claiming Jesus is son of God, and the Jews exaggerated by claiming Ezra is son of God. Muslims avoid all these errors by maintaining a middle path: respecting all prophets as infallible servants of God, neither slandering them nor elevating them to divinity.

الفجور

التقصير

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any criticism of prophets is acceptable or that some prophets can be sinful.
Correction: The passage shows that Muslims must respect all prophets as sinless and avoid slander like that of the Jews. Prophets are protected from sin and are chosen by God.

Checkpoint

A) That he was a prophet
B) That he was a righteous man only, denying his prophethood
C) That he was the son of God
D) That he committed immorality

Key Takeaway
Muslims must believe that all prophets are chosen and infallible, avoiding both slander and deification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually repeated a negative story about a prophet without verifying its truth? Will you now commit to defending their honor whenever you hear such slander?
?
Do you find yourself leaning towards either excessive reverence (like treating prophets as almost divine) or careless disrespect (like criticizing their actions)? What specific step will you take today to align your heart with the Quranic description of them as 'chosen servants'?
?
This passage reveals that both Jews and Christians fell into error regarding the prophets. Is there any prophet whose status you have diminished or exaggerated in your thoughts? How will you actively correct that belief?

Belief in the Resurrection and Its Rational Proof
The author affirms belief in resurrection, its rational proofs, and its role in distinguishing believers from libertines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and prophetic basis for belief in resurrection, its role in distinguishing believers, and the moral consequence of denying it.

Core Idea
Belief in resurrection is established by reason and revelation; denying it leads to moral chaos.

In this passage, the author consolidates the doctrine of resurrection (al-baʿth) by grounding it in both prophetic tradition and rational proof. He emphasizes that this belief is not merely theoretical but serves as a crucial boundary between the faithful and the irreligious. Furthermore, he warns that denying resurrection emboldens the lower self to sin without restraint.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's flow.
Notice the careful structure: affirmation, proof, function, consequence, and omission.
2
Identify the two types of proof
The passage mentions both revelation (from the Book) and rational proofs (ḥujaj ʿaqliyya). Mark these as separate but complementary foundations.
3
Observe the social function
Belief in resurrection separates believers from the irreligious, specifically the 'mad' and 'libertines'. Understand this as a distinguishing mark of faith.
4
Reflect on the consequence of denial
Denying resurrection empowers the soul that commands evil (al-nafs al-ammāra) by removing fear of accountability. Consider how this affects moral behavior.
The author sees denial as giving license to sin.
5
Understand the author's restraint
The author refrains from discussing the form of gathering and reward due to great disagreement and length. Appreciate that he focuses only on the essential belief.
This shows a focus on core doctrine over disputed details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْإِيمَانُ بِالْبَعْثِ فَقَدْ وَرَدَ بِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ وَنَطَقَ بِهِ مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ مِنْ كِتَابِهِ وَأَثْبَتَ ذَلِكَ بِالْحُجَجِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ ذِكْرُهُ قَبْلَ هَذَا، وَهُوَ أَمْرٌ بِهِ يَنْفَصِلُ عَنْ أَصْحَابِ الدِّيَانَةِ مِنَ الْمَجَانِ وَالْخُلَعَاءِ، وَأَنَّ كُلَّ مَنْ آمَنَ بِنَبِيٍّ مِنَ الْأَنْبِيَاءِ فَقَدْ صَدَّقَ بِالْبَعْثِ، وَفِي الْجَحْدِ بِهِ إِيمَانُ النَّفْسِ الْأَمَّارَةِ بِالسُّوْءِ عَنْ عَوَاقِبِ الْمُطَالَبَاتِ، وَتَسْوِيغٌ لَهَا عَامَّةَ مَا تَشْتَهِيهِ مِنَ الْفَوَاحِشِ وَتَنْجَذِبُ إِلَيْهِ هِمَّتُهُ مِنَ الْمَآثِمِ وَالْقَبَائِحِ. وَأَمَّا الْكَلَامُ فِي صُورَةِ الْحَشْرِ وَالثَّوَابِ فَفِيهِ بَيْنَ النَّاسِ اخْتِلَافٌ كَبِيرٌ، وَفِي الْأَقَاوِيلِ وَشُبَهِهَا وَكَيْفِيَّةِ وُجُوهِ رَدِّهَا وَتَحْقِيقِ مَا أَتَى بِهِ الرَّسُولُ صَلَوَاتُ جَمِيعِ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ طُولٌ فَأَعْرَضْنَا عَنْ ذِكْرِهَا.
As for belief in the resurrection, it has been reported by him, peace be upon him, and that which came from His Book spoke of it, and he established it with rational proofs as mentioned before this. And it is a matter by which one separates from the irreligious among the mad and the libertines. And that everyone who believes in a prophet from among the prophets has affirmed the resurrection. And in denying it there is strengthening of the soul that commands evil from the consequences of demands, and granting it license for all the abominations it desires and for that which its aspiration is drawn to of sins and vile deeds. As for discussion on the form of the gathering and reward, there is among people great disagreement, and concerning the sayings and their ambiguities and the manner of refuting them and verifying what the Messenger, may all the prayers of God be upon him, brought, there is length, so we refrained from mentioning it.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines belief in the resurrection (al-baʿth) and provides its proof. The author states that belief in resurrection is taught by the Prophet and explicitly stated in the Quran. Moreover, the Prophet established it using rational proofs (ḥujaj ʿaqliyya) as mentioned earlier. The author then highlights a key function of this belief: it distinguishes Muslims from the irreligious, specifically the majān (madmen) and khul‘ā’ (libertines) who deny accountability. He argues that anyone who believes in any prophet necessarily affirms resurrection, because all prophets preached it. Conversely, denying resurrection empowers the soul that commands evil (al-nafs al-ammāra bi’l-sū’) by removing fear of consequences, giving it license to indulge in all sorts of sins and lewd acts. The author then states that he will not discuss the detailed form of the gathering (ḥashr) and reward because of the great disagreement and the length required to refute the various views. This passage thus establishes the essential belief and its rational basis without delving into disputed specifics.

النفس الأمارة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in resurrection is purely based on revelation and has no rational basis.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that resurrection is established by both revelation and rational proofs (ḥujaj ʿaqliyya), making it a matter of reason as well as faith.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one consequence of denying the resurrection?
A) It leads to atheism
B) It strengthens the soul that commands evil
C) It causes one to reject all prophets
D) It leads to punishment in this world

Key Takeaway
Belief in resurrection is both a revealed truth and a rational necessity that distinguishes believers from libertines and restrains the lower self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe in resurrection in a way that restrains your desires, or do you behave as if there will be no accountability? Where in your life do you give your nafs free rein?
?
The author says denying resurrection empowers the soul commanding evil. Reflect on a recent sin you committed—did you act as if the consequences in the hereafter were unreal? What would change if you truly believed?
?
If belief in resurrection distinguishes you from the irreligious among the mad and libertines, does your daily conduct reflect that distinction? Name one specific area where your actions show you forget the resurrection.

The Wisdom of Fasting: Training the Soul
The author explains the rational benefit of fasting as training in self-control.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how fasting, by abandoning the greatest pleasures, cultivates gratitude, self-discipline, and trustworthiness.

Core Idea
Fasting trains the soul by conquering the strongest desires.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla reveals the profound wisdom behind the act of fasting. Far from being mere deprivation, fasting is a deliberate training of the soul. By voluntarily giving up the most powerful pleasures—food, drink, and marital intimacy—a person learns to master his desires and recognize God's blessings. The author explains how this discipline enables one to fulfill all trusts and avoid betrayal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on fasting's purpose
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice how the author begins by stating that fasting was brought by the Prophet in accordance with wisdom.
2
Identify the three pleasures abandoned
The passage mentions the greatest pleasures: those related to the preservation of the body (food, drink) and the continuation of offspring (marital relations). Recognize these as the objects of self-restraint.
These are the 'pleasures upon which the preservation of bodies and the production of offspring depend.'
3
Understand the purpose of experiencing bitterness
Reflect on why the author says fasting involves 'enduring the bitterness of losing them.' This leads to recognizing the greatness of God's blessings—a key aspect of gratitude.
4
See how training leads to trustworthiness
The passage argues that if one can control these powerful urges, then guarding against lesser sins becomes easier. Notice the vivid example of resisting a beautiful wife at the height of passion, delicious food when hungry, and cold water when thirsty.
This demonstrates that fasting builds a strong will.
5
Connect to the broader lesson
Conclude that fasting is not just about abstaining from food and drink; it is a comprehensive spiritual exercise that cultivates self-control, gratitude, and trustworthiness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثا: الصيام] وأما الصيام فقد ورد به النبي محمد عليه السلام على ما تقتضيه الحكمة من هجران الملاذ التي يتعلق بها بقاء الأبدان، وحصول النسل وهي أجل الملاذ وأعلاها قدراً، وهي في الأحايين ليعرف عند تجشم مرارة فقدانها عظم نعمة الله عليه بها، ثم عند الارتياض بهجرانها يقدر على أداء كل أمانة والصون عن كل خيانة إذ لا داعى في النفس إلى شيء من الملاذ يوازي الداعي إلى هذه، فإذا انقادت له نفسه للتحرز عن قضاء شهوته بحليلته الحسناء الرائع جمالها الفاشي غنجها ، ودلالها المساعدة له على ما يراودها المسارعة إلى ما يطالبها عند هيجان الطبيعة وتوقان الشهوة والامتناع عن الأطعمة الشهية مع دواعي النفس عند غلبة الجموح، والاتقاء عن شرب الزلال البارد عند شدة الظمأ وحر الهواجر ، ينقاد له للتصون عن كل ما تدعوه النفس إليه، ويزينه الشيطان ويسوله من الخيانة وترك الأمانة، وركوب المعاصي ومقارفة الفواحش. لذة المنكح والمأكل والمشرب ، وتوجب الحكمة الهجران عنها التهجد وصدقة الفطر] (۲) ثم شريعة الإسلام قرنت بالتهجد (۱) بالليل مصليًا، والإنفاق على (1) من يتصل به، والاعتكاف في المساجد، ثم قرن نهايته (۳) بعبادة (٤) تدل (٥) هيئتها (1) على عز الدولة ونباهة الملة، وهي الخروج إلى المصلى وشرف الجود بما يقتينه من المال وإغناء المحتاجين من عباد الله وذلك صدقة الفطر، وليس في دين من الأديان مثل هذا الصوم مع ما في تجشم مرارة الصوم معرفة ما يقاسيه الفقراء من الشدة فيصير ذلك باعثًا له على الإحسان إليهم والاعتناء بشأنهم ومواساتهم بما خصه الله من المال.
Third: Fasting. As for fasting, it has been brought by the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, in accordance with what wisdom requires: abandoning pleasures upon which the preservation of bodies and the production of offspring depend, and they are the greatest pleasures and the highest in rank. And it is at times so that by enduring the bitterness of losing them one may recognize the greatness of God's blessing upon him; then, by training through abandoning them, one becomes able to perform every trust and guard against every betrayal—for there is no motive in the soul towards any pleasure that equals the motive towards these. So when he subdues his soul to refrain from satisfying his desire with his beautiful wife, whose beauty is enchanting, whose coquetry is widespread, and whose flirtation assists him in what he solicits and hastens to what he demands—at the height of natural excitement and the surge of passion—and refrains from delicious foods with the soul's promptings at the time of overwhelming rebelliousness, and refrains from drinking cool fresh water during severe thirst and the heat of midday, then it is subdued for him to protect himself from everything that his soul calls him to and that Satan adorns and suggests to him of betrayal, abandonment of trust, committing sins, and perpetrating indecencies. The pleasure of marriage, eating, and drinking—wisdom requires abandoning them. [Subheading:] Night prayer and the charity of breaking the fast. Then the Islamic law paired [fasting] with night prayer, praying at night, and spending on one's relatives, and retreat in mosques. Then it paired its end with a worship whose form indicates the glory of the state and the nobility of the religion: namely, going out to the prayer ground and the honor of giving a portion of one's wealth and enriching the needy among the servants of God, and that is the charity of breaking the fast. And there is no religion that has anything like this fasting, along with the fact that in enduring the bitterness of fasting there is knowledge of what the poor suffer of hardship, so that becomes a motivator for him to do good to them, care for their affairs, and share with them out of the wealth God has given him.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a rational and scriptural exposition of the wisdom behind fasting and its associated acts. The author begins by stating that fasting involves abandoning the highest pleasures—those related to the preservation of the body (food, drink, and marital relations) and the continuation of offspring. This abandonment serves to make one grateful for God's blessings by experiencing their loss. Moreover, it trains the soul to be trustworthy and free from treachery, because the strongest desires are those for food, drink, and sex. If a person can control himself from these even when the natural urges are strongest—as described vividly: refraining from his beautiful wife at the height of passion, from delicious food when appetite is overwhelming, and from cold water in extreme thirst—then he can certainly control himself from lesser temptations like betrayal and sin. The author then mentions that Islamic law connects fasting with night prayer (tahajjud) and spending on relatives, as well as i'tikaf in mosques. At the end of Ramadan, it prescribes the Eid prayer, which demonstrates the glory of Islam, and zakat al-fitr, which enriches the needy. Finally, fasting cultivates empathy for the poor, motivating charity. The passage concludes that no other religion has a practice like this.

Key Terms
الصيام
هجران الملاذ
مرارة فقدانها
الارتياض
أداء الأمانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fasting is merely about hunger and thirst; its benefit is only physical or ritual.
Correction: The passage shows that fasting targets the strongest desires (food, drink, sex) and aims to build self-discipline, gratitude, and trustworthiness—a comprehensive spiritual training.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one purpose of experiencing the bitterness of losing pleasures through fasting?
A) To become weak and humble.
B) To recognize the greatness of God's blessings and train the soul for trustworthiness.
C) To earn reward in the afterlife only.
D) To become indifferent to all desires.

Key Takeaway
Fasting is a deliberate training in self-control by abandoning the strongest desires, leading to gratitude, trustworthiness, and moral strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time your desire for food or drink, even halal, overpowered your sense of discipline? What does this lesson reveal about the real struggle fasting is meant to train you for?
?
Consider the strongest desires you have. If you cannot control them even during Ramadan, what does that say about your ability to resist other sins? What will you do about it starting this Ramadan?
?
The author says that by enduring the bitterness of losing these blessings, you recognize their greatness. When did fasting truly make you grateful for a simple meal? If not, why do you think that is, and how can you change your approach to experience that gratitude?

The Associated Acts of Fasting: Night Prayer, Charity, and Social Solidarity
The author enumerates the acts prescribed alongside fasting and their collective benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic law pairs fasting with night prayer, i'tikaf, and zakat al-fitr to complete its spiritual and social benefits.

Core Idea
Fasting is completed by night worship and charity that uplifts the community.

In this continuation, Imam al-Nasafi explains that Islamic law did not leave fasting isolated but paired it with complementary acts of worship. The night prayer (tahajjud), spending on relatives, and retreat in mosques (i'tikaf) are linked with fasting. Moreover, the end of Ramadan is marked by the Eid prayer and the charity of breaking the fast (zakat al-fitr), which demonstrate the glory of Islam and provide for the needy. The passage also highlights how fasting cultivates empathy for the poor, motivating generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on associated acts
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the transition from the previous discussion of fasting's internal benefits to the external acts prescribed by Islamic law.
2
Identify the acts paired with fasting
The passage mentions: night prayer (tahajjud), spending on relatives (nafaqa), retreat in mosques (i'tikaf). Recognize each as a separate act that complements fasting.
3
Understand the significance of the end of Ramadan
The author points out that the end of the fasting month is marked by Eid prayer and zakat al-fitr. These acts symbolize the glory of the religion and provide for the poor.
4
Reflect on the empathetic purpose of fasting
The passage states that enduring the bitterness of fasting makes one aware of the poor's hardship, leading to charity and care. This is a key social benefit.
5
Appreciate the uniqueness of this system
The author concludes that no other religion has a practice like this—a complete system of worship, charity, and social solidarity centered around fasting.

?

?

?
